<p>The <b>United States presidential election of 1896</b> was the 28th quadrennial <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election" title="United States presidential election">presidential election</a>, held on Tuesday, November 3, 1896. It was the climax of an intensely heated contest in which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="History of the Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> candidate <a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">William McKinley</a> (a former <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_Ohio" title="List of Governors of Ohio">Governor of Ohio</a>) defeated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="History of the Democratic Party (United States)">Democrat</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Jennings_Bryan" title="William Jennings Bryan">William Jennings Bryan</a> (a former <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">Representative</a> from Nebraska) in one of the most dramatic and complex races in American history.</p>
<p>The 1896 campaign was a <a href="/wiki/Realigning_election" title="Realigning election">realigning election</a> that ended the old <a href="/wiki/Third_Party_System" title="Third Party System">Third Party System</a> and began the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Party_System" title="Fourth Party System">Fourth Party System</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> McKinley forged a conservative coalition in which businessmen, professionals, skilled factory workers, and prosperous farmers were heavily represented. He was strongest in cities and in the <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">Northeast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Upper_Midwest" title="Upper Midwest">Upper Midwest</a>, and <a href="/wiki/West_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="West Coast of the United States">Pacific Coast</a>. Bryan was the nominee of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_Democratic_Party" title="History of the United States Democratic Party">Democrats</a>, the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Party_(United_States)" title="People's Party (United States)">Populist Party</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Silver_Republican_Party" title="Silver Republican Party">Silver Republicans</a>. He presented his campaign as a crusade of the working man against the rich, who impoverished America by limiting the money supply, which was based on gold. Silver, he said, was in ample supply and if coined into money would restore prosperity while undermining the illicit power of the money trust. Bryan was strongest in the South, rural Midwest, and Rocky Mountain states. Bryan's moralistic rhetoric and crusade for inflation (to be generated by a money supply based on silver as well as gold) alienated conservatives. Turnout was very high, passing 90% of the eligible voters in many places.</p>
<p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1893" title="Panic of 1893">Panic of 1893</a>, the nation had been mired in a deep economic depression, marked by low prices, low profits, high unemployment, and violent strikes. Economic issues, especially <a href="/wiki/Tariffs_in_United_States_history" title="Tariffs in United States history">tariff</a> policy and the question of whether the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a> should be preserved for the money supply, were central issues. Republican campaign manager <a href="/wiki/Mark_Hanna" title="Mark Hanna">Mark Hanna</a> pioneered many modern campaign techniques, facilitated by a $3.5 million budget. He outspent Bryan by a factor of five. The Democratic Party's repudiation of its economically conservative <a href="/wiki/Bourbon_Democrat" title="Bourbon Democrat">Bourbon</a> faction, represented by incumbent President <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a>, largely gave Bryan and his supporters control of the Democratic Party until the 1920s, and set the stage for Republican domination of the Fourth Party System and control of the White House for 28 of the next 36 years, interrupted only by the two terms of Democrat <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a>.</p>
<p>This was the last presidential election where the party in control of the White House has changed hands but at the same time where at least one state has switched its electoral votes to back a major party candidate in opposition to the party that just won (in effect backing the losing major party presidential candidate for two consecutive presidential elections where the winning candidate in each election happened to be from a different party).</p>
<p></p>

<p>At their <a href="/wiki/1896_Republican_National_Convention" title="1896 Republican National Convention">convention</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" title="St. Louis, Missouri">St. Louis, Missouri</a>, held between June 16 and 18, 1896, the Republicans nominated <a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">William McKinley</a> for president and <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Garret_A._Hobart" title="Garret A. Hobart">Garret Hobart</a> for vice-president. McKinley had just vacated the office of <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_Ohio" title="List of Governors of Ohio">Governor of Ohio</a>. Both candidates were easily nominated on first ballots.</p>
<p>McKinley's campaign manager, a wealthy and talented Ohio businessman named <a href="/wiki/Mark_Hanna" title="Mark Hanna">Mark Hanna</a>, visited the leaders of large corporations and major banks after the Republican Convention to raise funds for the campaign. Given that many businessmen and bankers were terrified of Bryan's populist rhetoric and demand for the end of the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard" title="Gold standard">gold standard</a>, Hanna had few problems in raising record amounts of money. In the end, Hanna raised a staggering $3.5 million for the campaign and outspent the Democrats by an estimated 5-to-1 margin. This sum would be equivalent to approximately $85 million, according to the inflation calculator of the Bureau of Labor Statistics. Major McKinley was the last veteran of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> to be nominated for president by either major party.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Democratic_Party_nomination">Democratic Party nomination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Democratic Party nomination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1896_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1896 Democratic National Convention">1896 Democratic National Convention</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:16_to_1_..._the_speech_that_won_the_nomination_..._at_the_National_Democtratic_Convention_at_Chicago,_1896.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4510" data-file-width="2953" height="458" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/16_to_1_..._the_speech_that_won_the_nomination_..._at_the_National_Democtratic_Convention_at_Chicago%2C_1896.tif/lossy-page1-300px-16_to_1_..._the_speech_that_won_the_nomination_..._at_the_National_Democtratic_Convention_at_Chicago%2C_1896.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/16_to_1_..._the_speech_that_won_the_nomination_..._at_the_National_Democtratic_Convention_at_Chicago%2C_1896.tif/lossy-page1-450px-16_to_1_..._the_speech_that_won_the_nomination_..._at_the_National_Democtratic_Convention_at_Chicago%2C_1896.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/16_to_1_..._the_speech_that_won_the_nomination_..._at_the_National_Democtratic_Convention_at_Chicago%2C_1896.tif/lossy-page1-600px-16_to_1_..._the_speech_that_won_the_nomination_..._at_the_National_Democtratic_Convention_at_Chicago%2C_1896.tif.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>

<p>One month after McKinley's nomination, supporters of silver-backed currency took control of the Democratic convention held in Chicago on July 7–11. Most of the Southern and Western delegates were committed to implementing the "free silver" ideas of the Populist Party. The convention repudiated President Cleveland's gold standard policies and then repudiated Cleveland himself. This, however, left the convention wide open: there was no obvious successor to Cleveland. A two-thirds vote was required for the nomination and the silverites had it in spite of the extreme regional polarization of the delegates. In a test vote on an anti-silver measure, the Eastern states (from Maryland to Maine), with 28% of the delegates, voted 96% in favor. The other delegates voted 91% against, so the silverites could count on a majority of 67% of the delegates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>An attorney, former congressman, and unsuccessful Senate candidate named <a href="/wiki/William_Jennings_Bryan" title="William Jennings Bryan">William Jennings Bryan</a> filled the void. A superb orator, Bryan hailed from Nebraska and spoke for the farmers who were suffering from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic_depression" title="Economic depression">economic depression</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1893" title="Panic of 1893">Panic of 1893</a>. At the convention, Bryan delivered one of the greatest political speeches in American history, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross_of_gold_speech" title="Cross of gold speech">"Cross of Gold" Speech</a>. Bryan presented a passionate defense of farmers and factory workers struggling to survive the economic depression, and he attacked big-city business owners and leaders as the cause of much of their suffering. He called for reform of the monetary system, an end to the gold standard, and government relief efforts for farmers and others hurt by the economic depression. Bryan's speech was so dramatic that after he had finished many delegates carried him on their shoulders around the convention hall. The speech united the convention delegates and earned Bryan their presidential nomination. He defeated his closest competitor, former Senator <a href="/wiki/Richard_P._Bland" title="Richard P. Bland">Richard "Silver Dick" Bland</a> by a 3-to-1 margin. <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Sewall" title="Arthur Sewall">Arthur Sewall</a>, a wealthy shipbuilder from Maine, was chosen as the vice-presidential nominee. It was felt that Sewall's wealth might encourage him to help pay some campaign expenses. At just 36 years of age, Bryan was only a year older than the minimum age required by the Constitution to be president. Bryan remains the youngest person ever nominated by a major party for president.</p>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:157px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GoldDemocraticConvention.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="431" data-file-width="270" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/GoldDemocraticConvention.png/155px-GoldDemocraticConvention.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/GoldDemocraticConvention.png/233px-GoldDemocraticConvention.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9a/GoldDemocraticConvention.png 2x" width="155"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GoldDemocraticConvention.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The National "Gold" Democratic Convention</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The pro-gold Democrats reacted to Bryan's nomination with a mixture of anger, desperation, and confusion. A number of pro-gold Bourbon Democrats urged a "bolt" and the formation of a third party. In response, a hastily arranged assembly on July 24 organized the <a href="/wiki/National_Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="National Democratic Party (United States)">National Democratic Party</a>. A follow-up meeting in August scheduled a nominating convention for September in <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis" title="Indianapolis">Indianapolis</a> and issued an appeal to fellow Democrats. In this document, the National Democratic Party portrayed itself as the legitimate heir to Presidents <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Jefferson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Jackson</a>, and Cleveland.</p>
<p>Delegates from forty-one states gathered at the National Democratic Party's national nominating convention in Indianapolis on September 2. Some delegates planned to nominate Cleveland, but they relented after a telegram arrived stating that he would not accept. Senator <a href="/wiki/William_Freeman_Vilas" title="William Freeman Vilas">William Freeman Vilas</a> from Wisconsin, the main drafter of the National Democratic Party's platform, was a favorite of the delegates. However, Vilas refused to run as the party's sacrificial lamb. The choice instead was <a href="/wiki/John_M._Palmer_(politician)" title="John M. Palmer (politician)">John M. Palmer</a>, a 79-year-old former Senator from Illinois.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Simon_Bolivar_Buckner" title="Simon Bolivar Buckner">Simon Bolivar Buckner</a>, a 73-year-old former governor of Kentucky, was nominated by acclamation for vice-president. The ticket, symbolic of post-Civil War reconciliation, featured the oldest combined age of the candidates in American history.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:157px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PalmerBuckner1896button.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="872" data-file-width="756" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/PalmerBuckner1896button.png/155px-PalmerBuckner1896button.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/PalmerBuckner1896button.png/233px-PalmerBuckner1896button.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/PalmerBuckner1896button.png/310px-PalmerBuckner1896button.png 2x" width="155"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PalmerBuckner1896button.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Palmer/Buckner campaign button</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite their advanced ages, Palmer and Buckner embarked on a busy speaking tour, including visits to most major cities in the East. This won them considerable respect from the party faithful, although some found it hard to take the geriatric campaigning seriously. "You would laugh yourself sick could you see old Palmer," wrote lawyer <a href="/wiki/Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis" title="Kenesaw Mountain Landis">Kenesaw Mountain Landis</a>. "He has actually gotten it into his head he is running for office."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The Palmer ticket was considered to be a vehicle to elect McKinley for some Gold Democrats, such as <a href="/wiki/William_Collins_Whitney" title="William Collins Whitney">William Collins Whitney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abram_Hewitt" title="Abram Hewitt">Abram Hewitt</a>, the treasurer of the National Democratic Party, and they received quiet financial support from Mark Hanna. Palmer himself said at a campaign stop that if "this vast crowd casts its vote for William McKinley next Tuesday, I shall charge them with no sin."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> There was even some cooperation with the Republican Party, especially in finances. The Republicans hoped that Palmer could draw enough Democratic votes from Bryan to tip marginal Midwestern and border states into McKinley's column. In a private letter, Hewitt underscored the "entire harmony of action" between both parties in standing against Bryan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, the National Democratic Party was not merely an adjunct to the McKinley campaign. An important goal was to nurture a loyal remnant for future victory. Repeatedly they depicted Bryan's prospective defeat, and a credible showing for Palmer, as paving the way for ultimate recapture of the Democratic Party, and this did indeed happen in 1904.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Populist_Party_nomination">Populist Party nomination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Populist Party nomination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Populist candidates:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/William_Jennings_Bryan" title="William Jennings Bryan">William Jennings Bryan</a>, former U.S. representative (<a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">Nebraska</a>)</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Seymour_F._Norton&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Seymour F. Norton (page does not exist)">Seymour F. Norton</a> from <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, writer</li>
</ul>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Candidates_gallery">Candidates gallery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Candidates gallery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 136px">
<div style="width: 136px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 134px;">
<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WilliamJBryan1902.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="619" data-file-width="519" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/WilliamJBryan1902.png/201px-WilliamJBryan1902.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/WilliamJBryan1902.png/302px-WilliamJBryan1902.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/WilliamJBryan1902.png/402px-WilliamJBryan1902.png 2x" width="134"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<center><a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">Representative</a><br/>
<b><a href="/wiki/William_Jennings_Bryan" title="William Jennings Bryan">William Jennings Bryan</a></b><br/>
from <a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">Nebraska</a></center>
</div>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Several third parties were active in 1896. By far the most prominent was the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Party_(United_States)" title="People's Party (United States)">Populist Party</a>. Formed in 1892, the Populists represented the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Agrarianism" title="Agrarianism">agrarianism</a> (derived from <a href="/wiki/Jeffersonian_democracy" title="Jeffersonian democracy">Jeffersonian democracy</a>), which held that farming was a superior way of life that was being exploited by bankers and middlemen. The Populists attracted cotton farmers in the South and wheat farmers in the West, but very few farmers in the Northeast, South, West, and rural Midwest. In the presidential election of 1892, Populist candidate <a href="/wiki/James_B._Weaver" title="James B. Weaver">James B. Weaver</a> carried four states, and in 1894, the Populists scored victories in congressional and state legislature races in a number of Southern and Western states. In the Southern states, including Alabama, North Carolina, Tennessee, and Texas, the wins were obtained by <a href="/wiki/Electoral_fusion" title="Electoral fusion">electoral fusion</a> with the Republicans against the dominant Bourbon Democrats, whereas in the rest of the country, fusion, if practiced, was typically undertaken with the Democrats, as in the state of Washington.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> By 1896, some Populists believed that they could replace the Democrats as the main opposition party to the Republicans. However, the Democrats' nomination of Bryan, who supported many Populist goals and ideas, placed the party in a dilemma. Torn between choosing their own presidential candidate or supporting Bryan, the party leadership decided that nominating their own candidate would simply divide the forces of reform and hand the election to the more conservative Republicans. At their national convention in 1896, the Populists chose Bryan as their presidential nominee. However, to demonstrate that they were still independent from the Democrats, the Populists also chose former Georgia Representative <a href="/wiki/Thomas_E._Watson" title="Thomas E. Watson">Thomas E. Watson</a> as their vice-presidential candidate instead of Arthur Sewall. Bryan eagerly accepted the Populist nomination, but was vague as to whether, if elected, he would choose Watson as his vice-president instead of Sewall. With this election, the Populists began to be absorbed into the Democratic Party; within a few elections the party would disappear completely. The 1896 election was particularly detrimental to the Populist Party in the South; the party divided itself between members who favored cooperation with the Democrats to achieve reform at the national level and members who favored cooperation with the Republicans to achieve reform at a state level.</p>
<p>As a result of the double nomination, both the Bryan-Sewall Democratic ticket and the Bryan-Watson Populist ticket appeared on the ballot in many states. Although the Populist ticket did not win the popular vote in any state, 27 electors for Bryan cast their vice-presidential vote for Watson instead of Sewall. (The votes came from the following states: Arkansas 3, Louisiana 4, Missouri 4, Montana 1, Nebraska 4, North Carolina 5, South Dakota 2, Utah 1, Washington 2, Wyoming 1.)</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Silver_Party">Silver Party</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Silver Party">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Silver candidates:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/William_Jennings_Bryan" title="William Jennings Bryan">William Jennings Bryan</a>, former U.S. representative (<a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">Nebraska</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Henry_M._Teller" title="Henry M. Teller">Henry M. Teller</a>, U.S. Senator from <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a></li>
</ul>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Candidates_gallery_2">Candidates gallery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Candidates gallery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 136px">
<div style="width: 136px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 134px;">

</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 125.33333333333px">
<div style="width: 125.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 123.33333333333px;">
<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Senator_Henry_M_Teller.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="692" data-file-width="533" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Senator_Henry_M_Teller.jpg/185px-Senator_Henry_M_Teller.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Senator_Henry_M_Teller.jpg/277px-Senator_Henry_M_Teller.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Senator_Henry_M_Teller.jpg/370px-Senator_Henry_M_Teller.jpg 2x" width="124"/></a></div>

<div class="gallerytext">

<i>(declined)</i></p>
</div>
</div>

</ul>
<p>With the Republican Party platform calling for strong support for the gold standard, many western Republicans walked out of the Republican Convention and formed the <a href="/wiki/Silver_Republican_Party" title="Silver Republican Party">National Silver Party</a>. Many began to push for the nomination of Colorado Senator <a href="/wiki/Henry_M._Teller" title="Henry M. Teller">Henry Moore Teller</a>, the leader of the party, but with the nomination of <a href="/wiki/William_Jennings_Bryan" title="William Jennings Bryan">William Jennings Bryan</a> in Chicago and the adoption of his pro-silver platform, it was decided that they should unite behind the Democratic ticket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones262.E2.80.93263_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones262.E2.80.93263-11">[11]</a></sup> The Bryan campaign swept to victory across the Mountain states because the dominant issue in those thinly-populated mining areas was silver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Socialist_Labor_nomination">Socialist Labor nomination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Socialist Labor nomination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Labor_Party_of_America" title="Socialist Labor Party of America">Socialist Labor</a> Convention was held in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> on July 9, 1896. The convention nominated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Matchett" title="Charles Matchett">Charles Matchett</a> of New York and <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Maguire_(labor_activist)" title="Matthew Maguire (labor activist)">Matthew Maguire</a> of New Jersey. Its platform favored reduction in hours of labor; possession by the federal government of mines, railroads, canals, telegraphs, and telephones; possession by municipalities of water-works, gas-works, and electric plants; the issue of money by the federal government alone; the employment of the unemployed by the public authorities; abolition of the veto power; abolition of the United States Senate; women's suffrage; and uniform criminal law throughout the Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Prohibition_Party">Prohibition Party</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Prohibition Party">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Prohibition_Party" title="Prohibition Party">Prohibition Party</a> split into a "narrow gauge," or single-issue anti-liquor party and a "broad gauge" group (later known as the National Prohibition Party) that supported many reforms, including free silver. The split occurred when a motion for the party to endorse free silver, put forward by <a href="/wiki/Charles_E._Bentley" title="Charles E. Bentley">Charles Bentley</a>, was defeated by an overwhelming margin of 650-160. Although Bryan himself later became a champion of prohibition, his position was unknown in 1896.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id=".22Narrow_Gauge.22_Prohibition_nomination">"Narrow Gauge" Prohibition nomination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title='Edit section: "Narrow Gauge" Prohibition nomination'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>"Narrow Gauge" Prohibition ticket:</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaign_strategies">Campaign strategies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Campaign strategies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>While the Republican Party entered 1896 assuming that the Democrats were in shambles and victory would be easy, especially after the unprecedented Republican landslide in the congressional elections of 1894, the nationwide emotional response to the Bryan candidacy changed everything. By summer, it appeared that Bryan was ahead in the South and West and probably also in the Midwest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> An entirely new strategy was called for by the McKinley campaign. It was designed to educate voters in the money issues, to demonstrate silverite fallacies, and to portray Bryan himself as a dangerous crusader. McKinley would be portrayed as the safe and sound champion of jobs and sound money, with his high tariff proposals guaranteed to return prosperity for everyone. The McKinley campaign would be national and centralized, using the Republican National Committee as the tool of the candidate, instead of the state parties' tool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Furthermore, the McKinley campaign stressed his pluralistic commitment to prosperity for all groups (including minorities).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Financing">Financing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Financing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The McKinley campaign invented a new form of campaign financing that has dominated American politics ever since.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Instead of asking office holders to return a cut of their pay, Hanna went to financiers and industrialists and made a business proposition. He explained that Bryan would win if nothing happened, and that the McKinley team had a winning counterattack that would be very expensive. He then would ask them how much it was worth to the business not to have Bryan as president. He suggested an amount and was happy to take a check. Hanna had moved beyond partisanship and campaign rhetoric to a businessman's thinking about how to achieve a desired result. He raised $3.5 million. Hanna brought in banker <a href="/wiki/Charles_G._Dawes" title="Charles G. Dawes">Charles G. Dawes</a> to run his Chicago office and spend about $2 million in the critical region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Meanwhile, traditional funders of the Democratic Party (mostly financiers from the Northeast) rejected Bryan, although he did manage to raise about $500,000. Some of it came from businessmen with interests in silver mining.</p>
<p>The financial disparity grew larger and larger as the Republicans funded more and more rallies, speeches, and torchlight parades, as well as hundreds of millions of pamphlets attacking Bryan and praising McKinley. Lacking a systematic fund-raising system, Bryan was unable to tap his potential supporters, and he had to rely on passing the hat at rallies. National Chairman Jones pleaded, "No matter in how small sums, no matter by what humble contributions, let the friends of liberty and national honor contribute all they can."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Republican_attacks_on_Bryan">Republican attacks on Bryan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Republican attacks on Bryan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Increasingly, the Republicans personalized their attacks on Bryan as a dangerous religious fanatic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The counter-crusading rhetoric focused on Bryan as a reckless revolutionary whose policies would destroy the economic system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Illinois Governor <a href="/wiki/John_Peter_Altgeld" title="John Peter Altgeld">John Peter Altgeld</a> was running for re-election after having pardoned several of the anarchists convicted in the Haymarket bombings. Republican posters and speeches linked Altgeld and Bryan as two dangerous anarchists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The Republican Party tried any number of tactics to ridicule Bryan's economic policies. In one case they printed fake dollar bills which had Bryan's face and read "IN GOD WE TRUST...FOR THE OTHER 53 CENTS," thus illustrating their claim that a dollar bill would be worth only 47 cents if it was backed by silver instead of gold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_responses">Ethnic responses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Ethnic responses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Democratic Party in Eastern and Midwestern cities had a strong German Catholic base that was alienated by free silver and inflationist panaceas. They showed little enthusiasm for Bryan, although many were worried that a Republican victory would bring prohibition into play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The Irish Catholics disliked Bryan's revivalistic rhetoric and worried about prohibition as well. However their leaders decided to stick with Bryan, since the departure of so many Bourbon businessmen from the party left the Irish increasingly in control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Labor_unions_and_skilled_workers">Labor unions and skilled workers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Labor unions and skilled workers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Bryan campaign appealed first of all to farmers. It told urban workers that their return to prosperity was possible only if the farmers prospered first. Bryan made the point bluntly in the "Cross of Gold" speech, delivered in Chicago just 25 years after that city had indeed burned down:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>"Burn down your cities and leave our farms, and your cities will spring up again; but destroy our farms, and the grass will grow in the streets of every city in the country."</dd>
</dl>
<p>Juxtaposing "our farms" and "your cities" did not go over well in cities; they voted 59% for McKinley. Among the industrial cities Bryan carried only two (<a href="/wiki/Troy,_New_York" title="Troy, New York">Troy, New York</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fort_Wayne,_Indiana" title="Fort Wayne, Indiana">Fort Wayne, Indiana</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>The main labor unions were reluctant to endorse Bryan because their members feared inflation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Railroad workers especially worried that Bryan's silver programs would bankrupt the railroads, which were in a shaky financial condition in the depression and whose bonds were payable in gold. Factory workers saw no advantage in inflation to help miners and farmers, because their urban cost of living would shoot up and they would be hurt. The McKinley campaign gave special attention to skilled workers, especially in the Midwest and adjacent states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Secret polls show that large majorities of railroad and factory workers voted for McKinley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_fall_campaign">The fall campaign</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: The fall campaign">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:William-Jennings-Bryan-speaking-c1896.jpeg"><img alt="Three-quarters standing portrait of Bryan in a dark suit and white tie, with hands clasped before him, and with a serious and commanding expression" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2854" data-file-width="1931" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/William-Jennings-Bryan-speaking-c1896.jpeg/170px-William-Jennings-Bryan-speaking-c1896.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/William-Jennings-Bryan-speaking-c1896.jpeg/255px-William-Jennings-Bryan-speaking-c1896.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/William-Jennings-Bryan-speaking-c1896.jpeg/340px-William-Jennings-Bryan-speaking-c1896.jpeg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:William-Jennings-Bryan-speaking-c1896.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bryan's imposing voice and height made a deep impression on many who thronged to hear him.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Throughout the campaign the South and Mountain states appeared certain to vote for Bryan, whereas the East was certain for McKinley. In play were the Midwest and the Border States.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:287px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wjb1896.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="586" data-file-width="962" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Wjb1896.jpg/285px-Wjb1896.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Wjb1896.jpg/428px-Wjb1896.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Wjb1896.jpg/570px-Wjb1896.jpg 2x" width="285"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wjb1896.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bryan traveled 18,000 miles in 3 months, concentrating on the critical states of the Midwest.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Republican Party amassed an unprecedented war chest at all levels: national, state and local. Outspent and shut out of the party's traditional newspapers, Bryan decided his best chance to win the election was to conduct a vigorous national speaking tour by train. His fiery crusading rhetoric to huge audiences would make his campaign a newsworthy story that the hostile press would have to cover, and he could speak to the voters directly instead of through editorials. He was the first presidential candidate since Stephen Douglas in 1860 to canvass directly, and the first ever to criss-cross the nation and meet voters in person.</p>
<p>The novelty of seeing a visiting presidential candidate, combined with Bryan's spellbinding oratory and the passion of his believers, generated huge crowds. Silverites welcomed their hero with all-day celebrations of parades, band music, picnic meals, endless speeches, and undying demonstrations of support. Bryan focused his efforts on the Midwest, which everyone agreed would be the decisive battleground in the election. In just 100 days, Bryan gave over 500 speeches to several million people. His record was 36 speeches in one day in St. Louis. Relying on just a few hours of sleep a night, he traveled 18,000 miles by rail<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> to address five million people, often in a hoarse voice; he would explain that he left his real voice at the previous stops where it was still rallying the people.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Palmer_McKinley1896.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1690" data-file-width="1200" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Palmer_McKinley1896.png/140px-Palmer_McKinley1896.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Palmer_McKinley1896.png/210px-Palmer_McKinley1896.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Palmer_McKinley1896.png/280px-Palmer_McKinley1896.png 2x" width="140"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Palmer_McKinley1896.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The National "Gold" Democratic Party undercut Bryan by dividing the Democratic vote and denouncing his platform.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In contrast to Bryan's dramatic efforts, McKinley conducted a novel "front porch" campaign from his home in <a href="/wiki/Canton,_Ohio" title="Canton, Ohio">Canton, Ohio</a>. Instead of having McKinley travel to see the voters, Mark Hanna brought 500,000 voters by train to McKinley's home. Once there, McKinley would greet the men from his porch. His well-organized staff prepared both the remarks of the visiting delegations and the candidate's responses, focusing the comments on the assigned topic of the day. The remarks were issued to the newsmen and telegraphed nationwide to appear in the next day's papers. Bryan, with practically no staff, gave much the same talk over and over again. McKinley labeled Bryan's proposed social and economic reforms as a serious threat to the national economy. With the depression following the Panic of 1893 coming to an end, support for McKinley's more conservative economic policies increased, while Bryan's more radical policies began to lose support among Midwestern farmers and factory workers.</p>
<p>To ensure victory, Hanna paid large numbers of Republican orators (including <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a>) to travel around the nation denouncing Bryan as a dangerous radical. There were also reports that some potentially Democratic voters were intimidated into voting for McKinley. For example, some factory owners posted signs the day before the election announcing that, if Bryan won the election, the factory would be closed and the workers would lose their jobs.</p>
<p>Bryan's midsummer surge in the Midwest played out as the intense Republican counter-crusade proved effective. Bryan spent most of October in the Midwest, making 160 of his final 250 speeches there. Morgan noted, "full organization, Republican party harmony, a campaign of education with the printed and spoken word would more than counteract" Bryan's speechmaking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several of Bryan's advisors recommended additional campaigning in the Upper South States of Kentucky, West Virginia, Maryland and Delaware. Another plan called for a coastal tour from Washington State to Southern California. Bryan however, opted to concentrate in the Mid-West and to launch a unity tour into the heavily Republican Northeast. Bryan saw no chance of winning in New England, but felt that he needed to make a truly national appeal. On election day the results from the Pacific Coast and Upper South would be the closest of the election.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Results">Results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>McKinley secured a solid victory by carrying the core of the East and Northeast, while Bryan did well among the farmers of the South, West, and rural Midwest. The large German-American voting bloc supported McKinley, who gained large majorities among the middle class, skilled factory workers, railroad workers, and large-scale farmers. The national popular vote was rather close, as McKinley took 51% to Bryan’s 47%. In the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_college" title="Electoral college">electoral college</a> McKinley won in a landslide, receiving 271 electoral votes (61%) to Bryan's 176 (224 were needed to win).</p>
<p>The National Democrats did not carry any states, but they did divide the Democratic vote in some states and helped the Republicans to carry the state of Kentucky. Gold Democrats made much of the fact that Palmer’s small vote in Kentucky was higher than McKinley’s thin margin in that state. This was the first time a Republican presidential candidate had ever carried Kentucky, and they were not to do so again until <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">Calvin Coolidge</a> in 1924.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> From this, they concluded that Palmer had siphoned off needed Democratic votes and hence thrown the state to McKinley. However, McKinley would have won the overall election even if he had lost Kentucky to Bryan.</p>
<p>Mayor <a href="/wiki/Tom_L._Johnson" title="Tom L. Johnson">Tom L. Johnson</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleveland,_Ohio" title="Cleveland, Ohio">Cleveland, Ohio</a>, summed up the campaign as the “first great protest of the American people against monopoly – the first great struggle of the masses in our country against the privileged classes.”</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_results">General results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: General results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>McKinley received a little more than seven million votes, Bryan a little less than six and a half million, about 800,000 in excess of the Democratic vote in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1892" title="United States presidential election, 1892">1892</a>. It was larger than the Democratic Party was to poll in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1900" title="United States presidential election, 1900">1900</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1904" title="United States presidential election, 1904">1904</a>, or <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1912" title="United States presidential election, 1912">1912</a>. It was somewhat less, however, than the combined vote for the Democratic and Populist nominees had been in 1892. In contrast, McKinley received nearly 2,000,000 more votes than had been cast for <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Harrison" title="Benjamin Harrison">Benjamin Harrison</a>, the Republican nominee, in 1892. The Republican vote was to be but slightly increased during the next decade.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography_of_results">Geography of results</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Geography of results">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PresidentialCounty1896Colorbrewer.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1700" data-file-width="2800" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/PresidentialCounty1896Colorbrewer.gif/400px-PresidentialCounty1896Colorbrewer.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/PresidentialCounty1896Colorbrewer.gif/600px-PresidentialCounty1896Colorbrewer.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/PresidentialCounty1896Colorbrewer.gif/800px-PresidentialCounty1896Colorbrewer.gif 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PresidentialCounty1896Colorbrewer.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Results by county explicitly indicating the percentage for the winning candidate. Shades of red are for McKinley (Republican), shades of blue are for Bryan (Democratic), and shades of green are for "Other(s)" (Non-Democratic/Non-Republican).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>One-half of the total vote of the nation was polled in eight states carried by McKinley (New York, New Jersey, Pennsylvania, Ohio, Indiana, Illinois, Michigan, Wisconsin). In these states, Bryan not only ran far behind the Republican candidate, but also polled considerably less than half of his total vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vote4_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-vote4-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>In only one other section, in the six states of <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>, was the Republican lead great; the Republican vote (614,972) was more than twice the Democratic vote (242,938), and every county was carried by the Republicans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vote4_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-vote4-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/West_North_Central_States" title="West North Central States">West North Central</a> section gave a slight lead to McKinley, as did the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_States" title="Pacific States">Pacific</a> section. But within these sections, the states of Missouri, South Dakota, Nebraska, Kansas, and Washington were carried by Bryan.</p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/South_Atlantic_States" title="South Atlantic States">South Atlantic</a> section and in the <a href="/wiki/East_South_Central_States" title="East South Central States">East South Central</a> section, the Democratic lead was pronounced, and in the <a href="/wiki/West_South_Central_States" title="West South Central States">West South Central</a> section and in the <a href="/wiki/Mountain_States" title="Mountain States">Mountain</a> section, the vote for Bryan was overwhelming. In these four sections, comprising 21 states, McKinley carried only 322 counties and four states – Delaware, Maryland, Kentucky, and West Virginia.</p>
<p>A striking feature of this examination of the state returns is found in the overwhelming lead for one or the other party in 22 of the 45 states. It was true of the McKinley vote in every New England state and in New York, Pennsylvania, and Illinois. It was also true of the Bryan vote in eight states of the lower South and five states of the Mountain West. Sectionalism was thus marked in this first election of the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Party_System" title="Fourth Party System">Fourth Party System</a>.</p>
<p>This was the last election in which the Democrats won South Dakota until 1932, the last in which the Democrats won Utah and Washington until 1916, the last in which the Democrats won Wyoming until 1912, and the last in which the Democrats won Nebraska until 1908.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_votes">Southern votes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Southern votes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the South, there were numerous Republican counties, notably in Texas, Tennessee, North Carolina, Kentucky, and Virginia. Even in Georgia, a state in the <a href="/wiki/Deep_South" title="Deep South">Deep South</a>, there were counties returning Republican majorities. This was the result of an attempt by Republican politicians to heal sectional resentment and make the South competitive.</p>
<p>While traveling the South in 1895, McKinley realized that many conservative Southern whites were angry at the populist radicals who controlled the state's Democratic Party. White businessmen of Georgia wanted the support of a party that would oppose regulation and taxation as long as it would allow them to preserve <a href="/wiki/White_supremacy" title="White supremacy">white supremacy</a>. The possibility of preserving white supremacy without offending loyal black Republicans seemed like a possibility after black leader <a href="/wiki/Booker_T._Washington" title="Booker T. Washington">Booker T. Washington</a> gave a speech in 1895 that proposed the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlanta_Compromise" title="Atlanta Compromise">Atlanta Compromise</a>", which held that whites should support blacks in their struggle for economic independence as they learned trade and industry, and in return blacks would not challenge the political or social order of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jim_Crow" title="Jim Crow">Jim Crow</a>. The policy was somewhat of a success because McKinley won 37% of Georgia's vote (the closest a Republican would come to winning the state until <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1928" title="United States presidential election, 1928">1928</a>).</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Close_states">Close states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Close states">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Margin of victory less than 1% (26 electoral votes):</p>
<ol>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>Kentucky</b>, 0.06%</span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>South Dakota</b>, 0.22%</span></li>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>California</b>, 0.64%</span></li>
</ol>
<p>Margin of victory less than 5% (55 electoral votes):</p>
<ol>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>Oregon</b>, 2.09%</span></li>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>Indiana</b>, 2.85%</span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>Kansas</b>, 3.69%</span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>Wyoming</b>, 3.74%</span></li>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>Ohio</b>, 4.78%</span></li>
</ol>
<p>Margin of victory between 5% and 10% (66 electoral votes):</p>
<ol>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>Nebraska</b>, 5.35%</span></li>
<li><span style="color:red;"><b>West Virginia</b>, 5.40%</span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>Tennessee</b>, 5.76%</span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>North Carolina</b>, 5.82%</span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>Virginia</b>, 6.56%</span></li>
<li><span style="color:blue;"><b>Missouri</b>, 8.71%</span></li>
</ol>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_sources">Primary sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_presidential_election,_1896&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Primary sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Bryan, William Jennings. <i><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?vid=OCLC30986706" rel="nofollow">The First Battle: A Story of the Campaign of 1896</a></i> (1897), speeches from 1896 campaign.</li>

National Democratic committee.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AUnited+States+presidential+election%2C+1896&amp;rft.au=National+Democratic+Committee&amp;rft.btitle=Campaign+Text-book+of+the+National+Democratic+Party&amp;rft.date=1896&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2F%3Fid%3DVIwkzQzzbl4C%26vq%3Dwilson%26dq%3DDemocratic%2B%2522campaign%2Btext%2BBook%2522%26pg%3DPP19%26printsec%3D4%26lpg%3DPP19&amp;rft.pub=National+Democratic+committee&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span>
